Fronthaul C/U plane network 18
In slicing phase 1 and 2, slicing is not supported on O-RAN fronthaul components. In practical 19
terms this means O-RUs and O-DUs are common across all slices and there is a single 7.2X eCPRI 20
stream between the O-RU and O-DU which carries all the control and user plane traffic associated 21

O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        175
with all slices. The 7.2X C/U interface must support Ethernet encapsulation or optionally support 1
Ethernet / IP encapsulation.  Slicing phase 1 and 2 use cases assume the Ethernet variant of the 7.2X 2
protocol, meaning Ethernet connectivity needs to exist between O-RUs and the parent O-DU. 3
Slicing phase 1 and 2 use cases also assumes two radio deployments options:  4
Co-located O-RU and O-DU at cell site.   5
Ethernet connectivity needs to exist between the O-DU and the O-RUs and they both reside in the 6
cell site. There are two options: 7
• Direct point to point connectivity between the O-RU and the O-DU. In this case the 8
transport network plays no part in the connectivity or the provision of an Ethernet service 9
between the O-RU and the O-DU.  10
• Connectivity between the O-DU and O-RUs is via the “Cell Site Router” (CSR). In this case 11
the Cell Site Router provides layer 2 bridging, or an L2 Ethernet cross connect function 12
between the O-DUs port and the O-RUs port. See section 19.6 for more details. 13
O-RU and O-DU separated by access network 14
For distributed solutions, the O-RUs and the O-DUs are separated across the access portion of the 15
transport network. In this instance, an Ethernet service needs to be created across the MPLS or 16
SRv6 transport underlay. This Ethernet service can use either an E-Line or E-Tree service 17
configured using EVPN over MPLS or SRv6. See sections 13.2 for more details. 18
 19
Midhaul user plane network (F1-U and Xn-U) 20
Slicing Phase 1 21
In slicing phase 1 slicing is not supported on Midhaul O-RAN mobile components. O-DUs and O-22
CU-UPs do not have a concept of slicing with the F1-U traffic from all slices originating and 23
terminating on the same physical/logical interface and IP address on the O-DU.  In scenarios, where 24
the O-CU-UP is shared between two or more slices there is also no concept of slicing on the O-CU-25
UP. In situations, as shown in Figure 25-2, where the O-CU-UP is dedicated to a slice, then the O-26
CU-UP function will only receive and send traffic associated with that slice, however the O-CU-UP 27
slice assignment and selection process is completely transparent to the transport network and under 28
the control of the O-RAN / 3GPP control planes. For this reason, in slicing phase 1 a single MP-29
BGP L3VPN mapped to the underlay’s default routing algorithm is used across the transport 30
network to support all Midhaul user plane traffic regardless of slice it belongs too. The L3VPN 31
needs to be present on all TNEs and DC-TNEs that either connect directly or indirectly (through the 32
DC infrastructure) to mobile components that support Midhaul user plane interfaces. Access to the 33
Midhaul user plane VPN, on the TNEs, is via VLAN interfaces.   34
 A further challenge with Midhaul user plane interfaces in slicing phase 1 is the lack of a QoS 35
mapping function on the O-DUs to map radio orientated QoS parameters, controlled by the 3GPP / 36
ORAN control planes, to transport orientated DSCP QoS markings. The means the transport 37
network QoS mechanisms must treat all uplink traffic the same, regardless of the importance of the 38
data contained within Midhaul GTP-U packets.  39
 40
Slicing Phase 2  41
Midhaul slicing and QoS is supported in slicing phase 2. F1-U traffic segregation is supported using 42
a VLAN per user plane slice on the O-DUs and O-CU-UPs and QoS is supported by the O-DUs and 43
O-CU-UPs setting a DSCP value appropriate to the importance of the user payload in the GTP-U 44
packet.  45
O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        176
For slicing phase 2 this annex provides an example where midhaul and backhaul user plane traffic 1
associated with a slice shares a single MP-BGP L3VPN that provides any to any connectivity. If an 2
operator wishes to keep the midhaul and backhaul traffic separate then they could run each slice’s 3
midhaul and backhaul traffic in separate MP-BGP L3VPNs or alternatively use “Route Target” 4
(RT) and BGP community filtering within a single L3VPN, so only backhaul mobile components 5
can communicate with each other and only midhaul mobile components can communicate with 6
each other. Examples of how this can be done are in the following section. 7
  8
Backhaul user plane network (N3 and N9) 9
WG-1 slicing phase 1 and 2 includes user plane slicing in the backhaul network. In phase 1 and 2 it 10
is assumed traffic associated with different slices originates from individual logical Ethernet 11
interfaces on the O-CU-UPs backhaul interfaces and UPFs. These same slice mappings can be 12
maintained across the transport network by mapping VLAN attachment circuits to different 13
L3VPNs on the TNEs or DC-TNEs. Within the transport network MP-BGP L3VPNs can be built 14
per slice or shared between slices depending how the mobile component present the slice traffic to 15
the transport network. For slicing phase 1 and 2 these L3VPNs are mapped to the underlay’s default 16
routing algorithm but as the slice use cases becomes more sophisticated, then flex-algo or Traffic 17
Engineering can be used to map L3VPN to different underlay forwarding planes.     18
 Backhaul L3VPNs (phase 1) or backhaul/midhaul L3VPNs (phase 2) associated with a slice needs 19
to be present on all TNEs and DC-TNEs that either connect directly or indirectly (through the DC 20
infrastructure) to mobile components supporting that slice. Access to the slice’s L3VPN, on the 21
TNEs, is via an VLAN attachment circuit.   22
Backhaul Transport Slicing 23
A full description of MP-BGP based L3VPNs is contained in sections 13.3 and Annex C: MP-BGP 24
based L3VPNs. MP-BGP based L3VPNs support flexible connectivity models and are highly 25
scalable in terms of sites, VPNs and the number of VPN IP routes so able to  26
deal with transport connectivity requirements associated with WG-1s slicing phases and the 27
anticipated size of 5G environments. MP-BGP L3VPN’s have two basic mechanisms for 28
controlling connectivity within a L3VPN. 29
• Virtual Routing and Forwarding (VRF) instances: These are local routing table associated 30
with L3VPNs on Provider Edge (PE) TNEs. The VRF holds local and remote VPN routing 31
information. Local routes are those learnt by the PE and normally consist of attachment 32
circuits, routes learnt via routing protocols running over attachment circuits or via local 33
static routing. Remote VPN routes are those learnt from remote PEs. They are conveyed 34
between PEs using MP-BGP and held in the PE’s BGP table. Installation of remote routes 35
into the VRF routing table is determined by local policies. One of the key policy attributes 36
used to determine what routes are installed into a VRF are Route Targets (RTs). 37
• Route Targets (RTs): RTs are a BGP attribute attached to VPN routes when they are 38
distributed by MP-BGP from the PE where they were learnt. At the destination PE, filtering 39
of routes based on RTs is one of the key mechanisms for determining what remote VPN 40
routes are imported into a VRF.  41
 42
Slicing Phase 1 43
Slicing phase 1 supports three general purpose 5G backhaul service slices which are network wide. 44
For these simple slice use cases the design shown in Figure 25-11 could be used.  45

o VRF2: RT = MMTC 18
o VRF3: RT = NB-IOT 19

• This creates an any-to-any transport network allowing connectivity between all mobile 22
backhaul components in the slice.  23

The main differences are: 8

   14
Data Network (N6) 15
The data network or N6 is not part of the RAN and is not in scope of the WG-1 slicing work item. 16
However, transport slicing needs to extend to the N6 data network, particularly as the Data Network 17
(N6) network extends closer to the radio infrastructure with multiple breakout points and although 18
not discussed in this annex, the techniques outlined for the backhaul and midhaul user plane slicing 19
are largely applicable when building the data networks across a common packet switched transport 20
infrastructure. An important consideration when utilising multiple breakout points in the data 21
network (N6) is to ensure traffic exits the network in the desired place but also returning traffic 22
enters the data network (N6) in desired place. This requires UPF address block planning within the 23
data network, how these routes are advertised externally by the routers at the peering points and 24
how external routes are advertised within the Data network (N6).     25
 26
Transport Network Quality of Service architecture  27
Quality of Service (QoS) in the transport network in O-RAN  phase 1 and 2 is based on the Diffserv 28
architecture defined in RFC2475 [42] and further outlined in section 14 and Annex D: Quality of 29
Service of this document. The QoS architecture is widely implemented by Service Providers but 30

O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        179
there are significant differences between Service Providers in their QoS implementations. These 1
include DSCP bit usage, edge policies, marking strategies, numbers of queues on the edge and 2
within the core and how different Per Hop Behaviours (PHBs) are implemented. As such, this 3
section aims to outline some of the key considerations associated with 5G traffic and provide 4
example QoS implementations. Service Providers can use this information to adjust their existing 5
QoS architectures to support 5G.    6
Transport QoS considerations in a 5G environment  7
Section 7 outlines the 5G interfaces the transport network needs to support. In many areas the 8
transport QoS requirements for 5G are very similar to existing 4G and general multi-service 9
environments. However, there are some notable considerations and exceptions.   10
• In the context of packet-based switching technologies for O-RAN and 3GPP, the interplay 11
of QoS and transport slicing has two dimensions: 1) The QoS requirements of O-RAN 12
fronthaul traffic 2) The QoS requirements of the mobile services, which depends on the kind 13
of application being delivered to the end users. 14
The transport slicing and QoS architectures needs to consider both dimensions to properly 15
allocate QoS resources capable of satisfying the expected requirements for each kind of 16
traffic in terms of bandwidth, latency, etc. 17
• O-RAN 7.2X Fronthaul Traffic: The user and control traffic stream is eCPRI based and runs 18
between the O-RU and the O-DU. It is a new traffic type associated with 5G. The transport 19
requirements of the Fronthaul user and control plane interfaces are contained in the O-20
RAN.WG9.CTRP-REQ-V01.00 [18] but can be summarised as high bandwidth and more 21
importantly from a QoS perspective, extremely low delay (micro-seconds) traffic. Due to the 22
low delay requirements this traffic type will only ever be seen in the access network and 23
requires packet switching equipment to treat this traffic with the highest priority through the 24
device and when scheduling onto attached interfaces. Today, many Service Providers use 25
the highest priority queue for voice traffic, consequently, changes to the existing QoS 26
schemes may be required. 27
• 5G introduces Midhaul: Midhaul interfaces run between the O-DUs and the O-CUs and 28
between O-CUs. The F1 interfaces are a new set of interfaces associated with 5G, but use 29
GTP-U and GTP-C which is the same packet types used in Backhaul. It is expected that 30
Midhaul and Backhaul interfaces will have similar QoS requirements.      31
• 5G introduces new service types, each with their own QoS requirements.  GTPv1-U [5] is 32
used in the Midhaul and Backhaul for F1-U, Xn-U, N3 and N9 interfaces. The importance of 33
user data packet within the mobile environment is indicated in the QoS Field Identifier 34
(QFI)[13] in the GTP-U extension header[5]. High performance TNEs do not have the 35
capability to examine the QFI field and instead use the DSCP field in the IP header or the 36
MPLS Traffic Class to understand the QoS requirements of packets in the transport network. 37
For the transport network to be able to treat GTP-U packets in an appropriate fashion for the 38
mobile service, the Midhaul (starting from O-RAN slicing phase 2) and Backhaul (starting 39
from O-RAN slicing phase 1) mobile components (O-DUs, O-CU-UP and UPFs) must have 40
mechanisms to set a DSCP value in the GTP IP header appropriate to the QoS requirements 41
of the mobile service carried in the GTP-U packet. This could be achieved using a mapping 42
mechanism on the Midhaul and Backhaul mobile components that map QFI values to DSCP 43
values.    44
 45
NOTE: In O-RAN slicing phase 1, QFI to DSCP mapping facilities is a capability seen on Backhaul 46
mobile component’s (O-CU-UP (on backhaul side), UPF) user plane interfaces.  In phase 2, QFI to 47
O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        180
DSCP mapping capability and associated management and control is enhanced to Midhaul mobile 1
components (O-DU, O-CU-UP) user plane interfaces.   2
 3
3GPP QoS flows and Transport QoS  4
The concept of QoS in 4G LTE is based on bearers, while the concept of QoS in 5G mobile systems 5
is based on the QoS Flow concept [1] which provides finer granularity. Flows in 5G and 4G bearers 6
can be divided into guaranteed bit rate flows (GBR QoS flows) and non-guaranteed bit rate flows 7
(non-GBR QoS flows). 5G also has a new delay critical GBR flow category which is not present in 8
LTE. QoS flows also have a service expectation which is expressed in terms of packet delay budget 9
and packet error rate. The mobile QoS requirements are designated through the QoS Class Identifier 10
(QCI) in LTE [2] and the 5G QoS Identifier (5QI) in 5G [2]. These identifiers can be operator 11
defined but there is also a standardized list contained in [1]. Within the mobile domain traffic (of 12
flows in 5G or bearers in LTE) with a given QCI or 5QI should receive the same forwarding 13
treatment (e.g, in terms of scheduling, admission threshold, etc) 14
 15
NOTE: The majority of the QCI and 5QI are the same, with very few exceptions of identifiers 16
being defined in one case but not in the other (e.g., QCI 75 or 5QI 10). 17
 18
TNEs in the transport network use DSCP or MPLS traffic class markings in the IP/MPLS header to 19
determine QoS packet handling. To keep consistency of QoS treatment between the overlay mobile 20
service and the underlay packet-based switching networks it is interesting to look at the different 21
standard QCI and 5QI in qualitative terms to understand how distinct kind of flows (or bearers) at 22
the mobile overlay level could be grouped together and treated on the underlay packet transport 23
network. This equates to how different QCIs and QFIs could be mapped to DSCP markings on the 24
5G mobile components (RUs, DUs, CUs) and how these DSCPs are mapped to Diffserv Per Hop 25
Behaviour (PHB) in the transport network.     26
Figure 25-13 plots the standardized QCIs and 5QIs in relation to the defined packet loss and delay 27
characteristics (Note, the figure is not to scale). Each box in the plot firstly contains the 5QI or QCI 28
(top part) complemented with the values for delay / packet loss (at the bottom).  To distinguish 29
profiles, that are GBR, non-GBR or delay-critical GBR, a color code is used which is indicated in 30
the legend of the figure (note that delay-critical GBR 5QI in 5G are simply GBR QCI in LTE).    31
 32

O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        181
Figure 25-13: 5QI and QCI plot in relation to packet loss and delay  1
 2
The plot identifies 5QI or QCI values with common QoS flow characteristics. It is now possible to 3
group 5QI and QCI which require similar forwarding treatment in the transport network. Figure 4
25-14 presents a set of exemplary groupings, in which the 5QI/QCI are categorized into four 5
distinct groups: group 1 consists of QoS flows requiring low delay, e.g., < 30 ms; group 2 consists 6
QoS flows which are in the delay range between 50 and 75 ms; group 3 consists of the rest of GBR 7
5QI/QCI; while finally group 4, as default group, aggregates the rest 5QI/QCI of non-GBR profile. 8
 9
 10
Figure 25-14: Exemplary 5QI and QCI grouping 11
 12
Using the above example, a transport QoS architecture is illustrated in the next section that supports 13
a set of transport core PHBs capable of supporting existing services, new 5G services, 5G fronthaul 14
and protecting the infrastructure needed to deliver timing, OAM and the transport routing protocols. 15
 This annex makes no recommendations on QFI to DSCP mapping that needs to occur on the 16
Midhaul (starting from O-RAN slicing phase 2) and Backhaul (starting from O-RAN slicing phase 17
1) mobile components, as there are huge variations between operators on their usage of DSCP 18
markings. Viable approaches include: 19
• A dedicated DSCP marking for some 5QI / QFI value. 20
• Group 5QI and QFI values, for example as shown above, and allocate all 5QIs and QFIs in 21
the group the same DSCP value.  22
• A combination of the two.  23
 24
Regardless of the edge marking scheme chosen, the most important action occurs on the ingress 25
TNE which needs to map the edge DSCP marking to the appropriate transport core marking (MPLS 26
TC for MPLS underlays and DSCP for SRv6 underlays) to ensure the appropriate PHB in the core 27
of the transport network.     28
Transport QoS Architecture for O-RAN slicing phase 1 and 2 29
To ensure that Service Level Objectives (SLOs) requested for a particular slice are maintained, 30
appropriate QoS scheme must be deployed in the transport network. 31
 32

O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        182
The details of QoS functionality are provided in Annex D: Quality of Service. This section provides 1
guidelines about particular use of the QoS concepts discussed in that annex to deploy the QoS 2
architecture suitable for providing SLO guarantees for transport network slices. 3
 4
As outlined in Figure 25-15, the QoS model for transport network slicing has following major 5
building blocks: 6
 7
• Ingress (input) policing (rate limiting) at the edge of the transport domain 8
• Mapping function at the edge of the transport domain to map potentially large number of 9
edge traffic classes, as described earlier, into finite (typically 4 to 8) traffic classes used in 10
the transport core 11
• Flat egress (output) scheduling (queuing) on the transport network transit links in the 12
transport core with typically 4 to 8 traffic classes 13
• Egress (output) scheduling (queuing) at the edge – optionally hierarchical 14
 15
NOTE: Figure 25-15 is an example figure and for simplicity shows the traffic in one direction only 16
(from UPF on the left-hand side to O-CU-UP on the right-hand side). However, traffic is typically 17
bidirectional (for example UPF → O-CU-UP and O-CU-UP → UPF), therefore the building blocks 18
mentioned earlier are deployed in opposite direction, too. Thus, all transport transit interfaces 19
(transport core) should have flat egress (output) scheduling (queuing) deployed, whereas all 20
transport edge interfaces would have input policing and potentially hierarchical output scheduling 21
(queuing) deployed. Also, the figure shows only one type of interface (backhaul), in accordance 22
with O-RAN slicing phase 1 requirements. Similar QoS concepts apply to over sliced interfaces, for 23
example midhaul, starting from O-RAN slicing phase 2. 24
 25

Subsequent subsections provide detailed discussion for each element in the QoS architecture for 1
transport network slicing. 2
Edge QoS 3
When the slice is created, mechanisms required to implement the slice in the transport network are 4
deployed on the transport network elements (TNEs). An important building block is edge QoS to 5
condition slice traffic at the transport edge and ensure that slice traffic does not exceed the 6
previously requested and agreed bandwidth values.  7
Ingress policing 8
Ingress policing (rate limiting) is used to police (rate limit) the traffic of a particular slice when the 9
traffic enters the transport network domain. This slice admission control function is required to 10
ensure, that only traffic contracted by given slice at the time of slice creation (or at the time of last 11
slice modification) is accepted with SLO guarantees. 12
 13
NOTE: In the deployments where there is no expectation from the transport network to provide 14
SLO guarantees (delay, jitter, bandwidth, no traffic interference between different slices, etc.) 15
ingress policing might be omitted. 16
 17
Depending on the actual slice QoS profile requested at the slice creation/modification, out-of-18
contract traffic is either completely dropped at the transport network edge (if requested slice 19
guaranteed rate and maximum rate are equal, or maximum guaranteed rate is missing in the 20
request), or, if slice guaranteed rate is not equal to slice maximum rate, slice traffic is handled at the 21
edge in following way: 22
 23
• Slice traffic under guaranteed rate: unconditionally accepted at the transport edge 24
 25
• Slice traffic above guaranteed rate and below maximum rate: conditionally accepted at the 26
transport edge. Such traffic is marked at the transport edge with QoS marking (DSCP or 27
MPLS Traffic Class bits) representing high drop probability. Using Class based Weighted 28
Random Early Discard (CBWRED) functionality, described in Annex D: Quality of Service , 29
and implemented on transit transport network elements, when the transit links are nearing 30
the congestion (transmit queues on transit links are building up) CBWRED kicks in, and 31
starts to drop packets marked with QoS marking representing high drop probability. Thus, 32
when no congestion is observed in the transport network, slice traffic can go up to the 33
contracted value (no drop at the transport edge for traffic volumes below slice maximum 34
rate), but when congestion is close, high drop probability packets (above guaranteed rate but 35
below maximum rate) are being dropped at transit. This ingress marking behaviour at the 36
transport edge can be implemented with 2-rate 3-color policer. 37
 38
• Slice traffic above maximum rate (or above guaranteed rate, if maximum rate is not 39
specified during slice creation/modification): unconditionally dropped at the transport edge. 40
This behaviour can be implemented with 1-rate policer (when slice guaranteed rate equals to 41
maximum rate), or 2-rate 3-color policer (when different guaranteed and maximum rates are 42
specified for the slice) 43
 44
There could be many reasons for misbehaving (send traffic above the contracted rates) slices, like 45
for example: 46
 47
• Underestimate for slice traffic volume during slice creation (or slice modification) events 48
O-RAN.WG9.XPSAAS-v03.00

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        184
• Some failure within the slice, causing extra traffic generation 1
• Denial of Service (DoS) attack started from within the slice 2
 3
With slice admission control, implemented via ingress policing (rate limiting), it can be ensured that 4
only legitimate in-contract slice traffic is allowed at the transport edge, thus slices that are sending 5
traffic above the agreed rate do not negatively impact other slices. This provides traffic isolation 6
guarantees between the different slices. 7
 8
NOTE: In O-RAN slicing phase 1, only per slice SLO parameters might be specified. There is no 9
granular per class (per 5QI) SLO parametrization within each slice in phase 1. Therefore, in phase 1 10
of network slicing single policer (1-rate or 2-rate-3-color) per slice might be sufficient for slice 11
admission control implementation. 12
 13
O-RAN slicing phase 2 introduces slices with multiple traffic classes (multiple 5QIs) within a single 14
slice, like for example MBB with IPTV. Therefore, starting from slicing phase 2, more individual 15
policers, or even hierarchical policers, might be introduced to implement slice admission control. 16
 17
Ingress Edge to core traffic class mapping 18
As described earlier multiple 5QIs, resulting in multiple DSCP markings, are potentially being used 19
at the edge of the transport domain in the deployments. 20
 21
Transport core typically uses flat QoS model with up to 8 traffic classes. Therefore, mapping 22
function to map multiple transport edge classes (represented by multiple 5QI/DSCP values) to 23
limited set of transport core classes is implemented on the edge transport network element as 24
outlined in Figure 25-16. 25
 26
 27

class mapping is setting the Traffic Class (TC) field in outer MPLS label. If SRv6 is used as the 31

encapsulation mechanism, DSCP field in the outer IPv6 header carries the transport core class 1
marking, while DSCP field in the inner IPv4/IPv6 header, similarly to MPLS encapsulation, carried 2
transport edge class marking. 3
 4
Transport network elements (TNEs) associate transport core class markings, carried either in outer 5
MPLS header TC field, or in outer IPv6 header DSCP field, with QoS output queues and execute 6
appropriate scheduling mechanism to implement desired traffic prioritization during link congestion 7
events.  8
 9
Egress (hierarchical) scheduling 10
On the egress side of transport edge, egress scheduling is implemented to provide desired traffic 11
prioritization among the slices, as well as among traffic classes within each slice. Depending on the 12
hardware capabilities of the edge transport network element, as well as the intended granularity 13
(more “soft” or more “hard”) of the enforcement, one of the following option can be used: 14
 15
• flat, non-hierarchical QoS model with up to 8 queues (similar to transport core QoS model) 16
• hierarchical, slice-aware QoS model with separate set of queues per each slice 17
 18
As described in the Annex D: Quality of Service , the hierarchical QoS scheduler model allows for a 19
“hard” distribution of the bandwidth on an interface amongst the slices sharing that interface 20
capacity, protecting the use of the bandwidth for a given slice from the other slices on the same 21
interface. 22
 23
Core QoS 24
Transport core QoS is based on a flat model with 4 to 8 queues. Transport core QoS settings are 25
rather static in the nature, provisioned at the time the transport network infrastructure (interface 26
settings, IGP settings, BGP settings, etc.) is provisioned. Especially, there is no direct correlation 27
between transport core QoS and 5G slices being created/modified/deleted. Transport core QoS 28
doesn’t maintain any state related to individual 5G slice, neither adjust its QoS parameters based on 29
5G slices creation/modification/deletion events. 30
 31
The goal of transport core QoS is to ensure right prioritization of transport core traffic classes 32
during congestion events caused by network failures and subsequent traffic rerouting, so that during 33
such congestion events traffic classes requiring low latency are using priority forwarding, and 34
traffic classes requiring low drop are not dropped. The goal of transport core QoS is not end-to-end 35
bandwidth management or providing granular guarantees per each 5G slice. 36
 37
Table 9 contains a list of typical flows that can be observed in the multiclass transport network used 38
to transport 5G flows as well , taking into consideration s exemplary 5QI /QCI grouping described 39
earlier in this annex . When recommending appropriate transport core QoS model, overall QoS 40
policies for all flows must be taken into consideration. 41
 42

Table 9: Different flows per-hop latency/PDV (order of magnitude) 2
 3
Note 1: Per-hop latency includes all latency contributors, like frame transmission delay, self-queueing 4
delay, q ueuing delay , s tore-and-forward delay , as described in section 10.1.1.2.1. Exact per -hop 5
requirements depend on the overall network budget, number of hops, budget allocated to fibers, etc. 6
The table intends to emphasize only relative order of magnitude for per-hop latency/PDV to illustrate 7
the process of assigning traffic to QoS queues. 8
 9
Note 2: PTP unaware mode (i.e., router transiting G.8275.2 PTP stream, without T -TC function on 10
the router that could add timestamps to transit PTP packets) → strict-priority queue is required to 11
minimize jitter (actual latency value is not relevant, but its average should be constant). Minimizing 12
the latency via strict-priority queue minimizes jitter as well. 13
 14
Note 3: Max|TE| accumulated across the network must be ≤1.1 µs. 15
 16
Note 4: T-BC/T-TC (both G.8275.1 and G.8275.2) with physical layer time stamping → guaranteed 17
bandwidth queue is OK, strict -priority queue is not required, since jitter/PDV will be accounted by 18
physical layer timestamps in PTP packet. Also, latency value is not relevant, but average latency 19
should be constant. QoS should ensure that PTP packets are not dropped during congestion, and 20
guaranteed bandwidth queue is sufficient for that. 21
 22
Note 5: O-RAN/3GPP C-plane and M-plane kept in separate queue to ensure minimum bandwidth 23
guarantees during congestion events preventing failures of these planes, as well as to increase security 24
and separation of these planes. 25
 26
There are variety of QoS models, depending on the hardware support available on transport network 27
element platform. It is out of scope for this document to discuss all the various QoS models supported 28
by different hardware platforms of transport network elements. However, for illustration purposes, 29
two major, most common QoS models are worth to mention : with single priority queue, and with 30
multiple priority queues. 31

 1
 2
Figure 25-17: Example of QoS model with single priority queue 3
 4
Figure 25-17 outlines an example QoS model with a single priority queue. In this hardware model, 5
all flows with ultra-low latency/PDV sensitivity (PTP unaware mode, CPRI/RoE, eCPRI CU-plane) 6
must be placed in this priority queue, while other flows should be distributed among remaining 7
bandwidth queues. Bandwidth queue used for flows with low (but not ultra-low) latency/PDV 8
sensitivity (OAM with aggressive timers, latency sensitive U-plane and business traffic) should be 9
parametrized with relatively high weight used in WFQ/WRR/WDRR/MDRR (Weighted Fair 10
Queueing, Weighted Round Robin, Weighted Deficit Round Robin. Modified Deficit Round Robin) 11
scheduling algorithms, so that this queue is serviced very frequently, to avoid queue congestion and 12
to minimize latency/PDV. With high weight parameterization this queue behaves almost as priority 13
queue. 14
 15
To avoid starvation of the remaining queues during congestion events, priority queue, as well as 16
highly over dimensioned bandwidth queue must have a policer or shaper to limit the rate (PIR – 17
Peak Information Rate) of the queue. Queue buffer sizes in both cases must be aligned to maximum 18
latency requirements of the traffic assigned to the queue. 19
 20

 1
Figure 25-18: Example of QoS model with multiple priority queues 2
 3
Figure 25-18 outlines an example of queue assignment on hardware platforms supporting multiple 4
priority queues, dequeued in strict priority order. With multiple priority queues available it is 5
recommended to place PTP packets in unaware mode in the highest priority queue, to minimize the 6
PDV of these packets to the highest possible degree. Putting these packets above CPRI(RoE) or 7
eCPRI has only minimal influence on CPRI/eCPRI packets PDV, since PTP packets are very small 8
(~100 bytes). For example, serialization delay of such small packet on 10 GE interface is only 80 9
ns, so PDV factor contributing to CPRI/eCPRI PDV is very small as well and can be easily handled 10
by the CPRI/eCPRI reassembly functions. 11
